<p>Anarchists commonly employ <a href="/wiki/Direct_action" title="Direct action">direct action</a>. This can take the form of disrupting and protesting against unjust <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchy</a>, or the form of self-managing their lives through the creation of counter-institutions such as communes and non-hierarchical collectives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams20184–5_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams20184–5-119">[116]</a></sup> Decision-making is often handled in an anti-authoritarian way, with everyone having equal say in each decision, an approach known as <a href="/wiki/Horizontalism" title="Horizontalism">horizontalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019109–117_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019109–117-135">[132]</a></sup> Contemporary-era anarchists have been engaging with various <a href="/wiki/Grassroots" title="Grassroots">grassroots</a> movements that are more or less based on horizontalism, although not explicitly anarchist, respecting personal autonomy and participating in mass activism such as strikes and demonstrations. In contrast with the <i>big-A anarchism</i> of the classical era, the newly coined term <i>small-a anarchism</i> signals their tendency not to base their thoughts and actions on classical-era anarchism or to refer to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_anarchists" title="Classical anarchists">classical anarchists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a> to justify their opinions. Those anarchists would rather base their thought and praxis on their own experience which they will later theorize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinna2019145–149_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinna2019145–149-136">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>The decision-making process of small anarchist <a href="/wiki/Affinity_group" title="Affinity group">affinity groups</a> plays a significant tactical role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019109,_119_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019109,_119-137">[134]</a></sup> Anarchists have employed various methods in order to build a rough consensus among members of their group without the need of a leader or a leading group. One way is for an individual from the group to play the role of facilitator to help achieve a consensus without taking part in the discussion themselves or promoting a specific point. Minorities usually accept rough consensus, except when they feel the proposal contradicts anarchist ethics, goals and values. Anarchists usually form small groups (5–20 individuals) to enhance autonomy and friendships among their members. These kinds of groups more often than not interconnect with each other, forming larger networks. Anarchists still support and participate in strikes, especially <a href="/wiki/Wildcat_strike_action" title="Wildcat strike action">wildcat strikes</a> as these are leaderless strikes not organised centrally by a syndicate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019119–121_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019119–121-138">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>As in the past, newspapers and journals are used, and anarchists have gone <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Online" title="Online">online</a> in the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Web" title="World Wide Web">World Wide Web</a> to spread their message. Anarchists have found it easier to create websites because of distributional and other difficulties, hosting electronic libraries and other portals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019118–119_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019118–119-139">[136]</a></sup> Anarchists were also involved in developing various software that are available for free. The way these hacktivists work to develop and distribute resembles the anarchist ideals, especially when it comes to preserving users' privacy from state surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019120–121_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019120–121-140">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anarchists organize themselves to <a href="/wiki/Squatting" title="Squatting">squat</a> and reclaim public spaces. During important events such as protests and when spaces are being occupied, they are often called <a href="/wiki/Temporary_Autonomous_Zone" title="Temporary Autonomous Zone">Temporary Autonomous Zones</a> (TAZ), spaces where art, poetry, and <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">surrealism</a> are blended to display the anarchist ideal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinna2019139Mattern2019596Williams20185–6_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinna2019139Mattern2019596Williams20185–6-141">[138]</a></sup> As seen by anarchists, squatting is a way to regain urban space from the capitalist market, serving pragmatical needs and also being an exemplary direct action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinna2012250Williams2019119_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinna2012250Williams2019119-142">[139]</a></sup> Acquiring space enables anarchists to experiment with their ideas and build social bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019122_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams2019122-143">[140]</a></sup> Adding up these tactics while having in mind that not all anarchists share the same attitudes towards them, along with various forms of protesting at highly symbolic events, make up a <a href="/wiki/Carnivalesque" title="Carnivalesque">carnivalesque</a> atmosphere that is part of contemporary anarchist vividity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorland200437–38_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorland200437–38-144">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Key_issues">Key issues</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Issues_in_anarchism" title="Issues in anarchism">Issues in anarchism</a></div>
<p>As anarchism is a <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that embodies many diverse attitudes, tendencies, and schools of thought; disagreement over questions of values, ideology, and tactics is common. Its diversity has led to widely different uses of identical terms among different anarchist traditions which has created a number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definitional_concerns_in_anarchist_theory" title="Definitional concerns in anarchist theory">definitional concerns in anarchist theory</a>. The compatibility of <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_capitalism" title="Anarchism and capitalism">capitalism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1993565Honderich199531Meltzer200050Goodway20064Newman201053_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1993565Honderich199531Meltzer200050Goodway20064Newman201053-145">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_nationalism" title="Anarchism and nationalism">nationalism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_religion" title="Anarchism and religion">religion</a> with anarchism is widely disputed, and anarchism enjoys complex relationships with ideologies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_communism" title="Anarchism and communism">communism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collectivist_anarchism" title="Collectivist anarchism">collectivism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_Marxism" title="Anarchism and Marxism">Marxism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_unionism" title="Trade unionism">trade unionism</a>. Anarchists may be motivated by <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">divine authority</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_self-interest" title="Enlightened self-interest">enlightened self-interest</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veganarchism" title="Veganarchism">veganism</a>, or any number of alternative ethical doctrines. Phenomena such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civilisation" title="Civilisation">civilisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> (e.g. within <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-primitivism" title="Anarcho-primitivism">anarcho-primitivism</a>), and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_statist_democracy" title="Anarchism and statist democracy">democratic process</a> may be sharply criticised within some anarchist tendencies and simultaneously lauded in others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_George200531–32_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_George200531–32-146">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Gender.2C_sexuality.2C_and_free_love"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender,_sexuality,_and_free_love">Gender, sexuality, and free love</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_issues_related_to_love_and_sex" title="Anarchism and issues related to love and sex">Anarchism and issues related to love and sex</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emilearmand01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="199" decoding="async" height="427" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Emilearmand01.jpg/170px-Emilearmand01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Emilearmand01.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emilearmand01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Armand" title="Émile Armand">Émile Armand</a> was a French individualist anarchist who propounded the virtues of free love in the Parisian anarchist milieu of the early 20th century</div></div></div>
<p>As gender and sexuality carry along them dynamics of hierarchy, many anarchists address, analyse, and oppose the suppression of one's autonomy imposed by gender roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholas2019603_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholas2019603-147">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AnarchaFeminism.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2080" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/AnarchaFeminism.png/260px-AnarchaFeminism.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/AnarchaFeminism.png/390px-AnarchaFeminism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/AnarchaFeminism.png/520px-AnarchaFeminism.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AnarchaFeminism.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Collection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcha-feminist" title="Anarcha-feminist">anarcha-feminist</a> protests, symbols, and flags</div></div></div>
<p>Sexuality was not often discussed by classical anarchists but the few that did felt that an anarchist society would lead to sexuality naturally developing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucy2020162_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucy2020162-148">[145]</a></sup> Sexual violence was a concern for anarchists such as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Tucker" title="Benjamin Tucker">Benjamin Tucker</a>, who opposed age of consent laws, believing they would benefit predatory men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucy2020178_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucy2020178-149">[146]</a></sup> A historical current that arose and flourished during 1890 and 1920 within anarchism was <a href="/wiki/Free_love" title="Free love">free love</a>. In contemporary anarchism, this current survives as a tendency to support <a href="/wiki/Polyamory" title="Polyamory">polyamory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queer_anarchism" title="Queer anarchism">queer anarchism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholas2019611JeppesenNazar2012175–176_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholas2019611JeppesenNazar2012175–176-150">[147]</a></sup> Free love advocates were against marriage, which they saw as a way of men imposing authority over women, largely because marriage law greatly favoured the power of men. The notion of free love was much broader and included a critique of the established order that limited women's sexual freedom and pleasure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeppesenNazar2012175–176_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeppesenNazar2012175–176-151">[148]</a></sup> Those free love movements contributed to the establishment of communal houses, where large groups of travelers, anarchists and other activists slept in beds together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeppesenNazar2012177_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeppesenNazar2012177-152">[149]</a></sup> Free love had roots both in Europe and the United States; however, some anarchists struggled with the jealousy that arose from free love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeppesenNazar2012175–177_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeppesenNazar2012175–177-153">[150]</a></sup> Anarchist feminists were advocates of free love, against marriage, and pro-choice (utilising a contemporary term), and had a similar agenda. Anarchist and non-anarchist feminists differed on <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">suffrage</a> but were supportive of one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinna2019166–167_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinna2019166–167-154">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the second half of the 20th century, anarchism intermingled with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_wave_of_feminism" title="Second wave of feminism">second wave of feminism</a>, radicalising some currents of the feminist movement and being influenced as well. By the latest decades of the 20th century, anarchists and feminists were advocating for the rights and autonomy of women, gays, queers and other marginalised groups, with some feminist thinkers suggesting a fusion of the two currents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholas2019609–611_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholas2019609–611-155">[152]</a></sup> With the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_wave_of_feminism" title="Third wave of feminism">third wave of feminism</a>, sexual identity and compulsory heterosexuality became a subject of study for anarchists, yielding a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-structuralist" title="Post-structuralist">post-structuralist</a> critique of sexual normality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholas2019610–611_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholas2019610–611-156">[153]</a></sup> Some anarchists distanced themselves from this line of thinking, suggesting that it leaned towards an individualism that was dropping the cause of social liberation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholas2019616–617_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholas2019616–617-157">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_education" title="Anarchism and education">Anarchism and education</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="border: none; background: none; float: right;">
<caption>Anarchist vs. statist perspectives on education<br/><small>Ruth Kinna (2019)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinna201997_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinna201997-158">[155]</a></sup></small>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th scope="col">
</th>
<th scope="col">Anarchist education
</th>
<th scope="col">State education
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Concept</td>
<td>Education as self-mastery</td>
<td>Education as service
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Management</td>
<td>Community based</td>
<td>State run
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Methods</td>
<td>Practice-based learning</td>
<td>Vocational training
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Aims</td>
<td>Being a critical member of society</td>
<td>Being a productive member of society
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The interest of anarchists in education stretches back to the first emergence of classical anarchism. Anarchists consider proper education, one which sets the foundations of the future autonomy of the individual and the society, to be an act of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_aid_(organization_theory)" title="Mutual aid (organization theory)">mutual aid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinna201983–85_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinna201983–85-159">[156]</a></sup> Anarchist writers such as <a href="/wiki/William_Godwin" title="William Godwin">William Godwin</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_Justice" title="Political Justice">Political Justice</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Max_Stirner" title="Max Stirner">Max Stirner</a> ("<a href="/wiki/The_False_Principle_of_Our_Education" title="The False Principle of Our Education">The False Principle of Our Education</a>") attacked both state education and private education as another means by which the ruling class replicate their privileges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuissa2019514,_521Kinna201983–86Marshall1993222_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuissa2019514,_521Kinna201983–86Marshall1993222-160">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1901, Catalan anarchist and free thinker <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Ferrer" title="Francisco Ferrer">Francisco Ferrer</a> established the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Escuela_Moderna" title="Escuela Moderna">Escuela Moderna</a> in Barcelona as an opposition to the established education system which was dictated largely by the Catholic Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuissa2019511–512_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuissa2019511–512-161">[158]</a></sup> Ferrer's approach was secular, rejecting both state and church involvement in the educational process whilst giving pupils large amounts of autonomy in planning their work and attendance. Ferrer aimed to educate the working class and explicitly sought to foster <a href="/wiki/Class_consciousness" title="Class consciousness">class consciousness</a> among students. The school closed after constant harassment by the state and Ferrer was later arrested. Nonetheless, his ideas formed the inspiration for a series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_School_(United_States)" title="Modern School (United States)">modern schools</a> around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuissa2019511–514_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuissa2019511–514-162">[159]</a></sup> Christian anarchist <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>, who published the essay <i>Education and Culture</i>, also established a similar school with its founding principle being that "for education to be effective it had to be free."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuissa2019517–518_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuissa2019517–518-163">[160]</a></sup> In a similar token, A. S. Neill founded what became the <a href="/wiki/Summerhill_School" title="Summerhill School">Summerhill School</a> in 1921, also declaring being free from coercion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuissa2019518–519_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuissa2019518–519-164">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anarchist education is based largely on the idea that a child's right to develop freely and without manipulation ought to be respected and that rationality would lead children to morally good conclusions; however, there has been little consensus among anarchist figures as to what constitutes manipulation. Ferrer believed that moral indoctrination was necessary and explicitly taught pupils that equality, liberty and social justice were not possible under capitalism, along with other critiques of government and nationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAvrich19803–33Suissa2019519–522_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAvrich19803–33Suissa2019519–522-165">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Late 20th century and contemporary anarchist writers (<a href="/wiki/Paul_Goodman" title="Paul Goodman">Paul Goodman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Read" title="Herbert Read">Herbert Read</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Colin_Ward" title="Colin Ward">Colin Ward</a>) intensified and expanded the anarchist critique of state education, largely focusing on the need for a system that focuses on children's creativity rather than on their ability to attain a career or participate in <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a> as part of a consumer society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinna201989–96_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinna201989–96-166">[163]</a></sup> Contemporary anarchists such as Ward claim that state education serves to perpetuate socioeconomic inequality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard197339–48_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard197339–48-167">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>While few anarchist education institutions have survived to the modern-day, major tenets of anarchist schools, among them respect for child autonomy and relying on reasoning rather than indoctrination as a teaching method, have spread among mainstream educational institutions. Judith Suissa names three schools as explicitly anarchists schools, namely the Free Skool Santa Cruz in the United States which is part of a wider American-Canadian network of schools, the Self-Managed Learning College in Brighton, England, and the Paideia School in Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuissa2019523–526_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuissa2019523–526-168">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_state">The state</span></h3>
<p>Objection to the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> and its institutions is a <i><a href="/wiki/Sine_qua_non" title="Sine qua non">sine qua non</a></i> of anarchism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarter197114Jun201929–30_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarter197114Jun201929–30-169">[166]</a></sup> Anarchists consider the state as a tool of domination and believe it to be illegitimate regardless of its political tendencies. Instead of people being able to control the aspects of their life, major decisions are taken by a small elite. Authority ultimately rests solely on power, regardless of whether that power is <a href="/wiki/Open_government" title="Open government">open</a> or <a href="/wiki/Transparency_(behavior)" title="Transparency (behavior)">transparent</a>, as it still has the ability to coerce people. Another anarchist argument against states is that the people constituting a government, even the most altruistic among officials, will unavoidably seek to gain more power, leading to corruption. Anarchists consider the idea that the state is the collective will of the people to be an unachievable fiction due to the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Ruling_class" title="Ruling class">ruling class</a> is distinct from the rest of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJun201932–38_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJun201932–38-170">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>Specific anarchist attitudes towards the state vary. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Paul_Wolff" title="Robert Paul Wolff">Robert Paul Wolff</a> believed that the tension between authority and autonomy would mean the state could never be legitimate. Bakunin saw the state as meaning "coercion, domination by means of coercion, camouflaged if possible but unceremonious and overt if need be." <a href="/wiki/A._John_Simmons" title="A. John Simmons">A. John Simmons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Green_(philosopher)" title="Leslie Green (philosopher)">Leslie Green</a>, who leaned toward philosophical anarchism, believed that the state could be legitimate if it is governed by consensus, although they saw this as highly unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWendt20202Ashwood2018727_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWendt20202Ashwood2018727-171">[168]</a></sup> Beliefs on how to abolish the state also differ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshwood2018735_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshwood2018735-172">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_arts">The arts</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_the_arts" title="Anarchism and the arts">Anarchism and the arts</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apple_Harvest_by_Camille_Pissarro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6960" data-file-width="8501" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Apple_Harvest_by_Camille_Pissarro.jpg/340px-Apple_Harvest_by_Camille_Pissarro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Apple_Harvest_by_Camille_Pissarro.jpg/510px-Apple_Harvest_by_Camille_Pissarro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Apple_Harvest_by_Camille_Pissarro.jpg/680px-Apple_Harvest_by_Camille_Pissarro.jpg 2x" width="340"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apple_Harvest_by_Camille_Pissarro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Les chataigniers a Osny</i> (1888) by anarchist painter Camille Pissarro is a notable example of blending anarchism and the arts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntliff199899_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntliff199899-173">[170]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The connection between anarchism and art was quite profound during the classical era of anarchism, especially among artistic currents that were developing during that era such as futurists, surrealists and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMattern2019592_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMattern2019592-174">[171]</a></sup> In literature, anarchism was mostly associated with the <a href="/wiki/New_Apocalyptics" title="New Apocalyptics">New Apocalyptics</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Neo-romanticism" title="Neo-romanticism">neo-romanticism</a> movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGifford2019577_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGifford2019577-175">[172]</a></sup> In music, anarchism has been associated with music scenes such as punk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1993493–494Dunn2012EvrenKinnaRouselle2013_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1993493–494Dunn2012EvrenKinnaRouselle2013-176">[173]</a></sup> Anarchists such as <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Read" title="Herbert Read">Herbert Read</a> stated that the border between the artist and the non-artist, what separates art from a daily act, is a construct produced by the alienation caused by capitalism and it prevents humans from living a joyful life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMattern2019592–593_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMattern2019592–593-177">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other anarchists advocated for or used art as a means to achieve anarchist ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMattern2019593_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMattern2019593-178">[175]</a></sup> In his book <i>Breaking the Spell: A History of Anarchist Filmmakers, Videotape Guerrillas, and Digital Ninjas</i>, Chris Robé claims that "anarchist-inflected practices have increasingly structured movement-based video activism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobé201744_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobé201744-179">[176]</a></sup> Throughout the 20th century, many prominent anarchists (<a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emma_Goldman" title="Emma Goldman">Emma Goldman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Landauer" title="Gustav Landauer">Gustav Landauer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camillo_Berneri" title="Camillo Berneri">Camillo Berneri</a>) and publications such as <i><a href="/wiki/Anarchy_(magazine)" title="Anarchy (magazine)">Anarchy</a></i> wrote about matters pertaining to the arts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillerDirlikRosemontAugustyn20191_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillerDirlikRosemontAugustyn20191-180">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Three overlapping properties made art useful to anarchists. It could depict a critique of existing society and hierarchies, serve as a prefigurative tool to reflect the anarchist ideal society and even turn into a means of direct action such as in protests. As it appeals to both emotion and reason, art could appeal to the whole human and have a powerful effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMattern2019593–596_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMattern2019593–596-181">[178]</a></sup> The 19th-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-impressionist" title="Neo-impressionist">neo-impressionist</a> movement had an ecological aesthetic and offered an example of an anarchist perception of the road towards socialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntliff199878_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntliff199878-182">[179]</a></sup> In <i>Les chataigniers a Osny</i> by anarchist painter <a href="/wiki/Camille_Pissarro" title="Camille Pissarro">Camille Pissarro</a>, the blending of aesthetic and social harmony is prefiguring an ideal anarchistic agrarian community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntliff199899_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntliff199899-173">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2>
<p>The most common critique of anarchism is that humans cannot <a href="/wiki/Self-governance" title="Self-governance">self-govern</a> and so a state is necessary for human survival. Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> supported this critique, stating that "[p]eace and war, tariffs, regulations of sanitary conditions and the sale of noxious drugs, the preservation of a just system of distribution: these, among others, are functions which could hardly be performed in a community in which there was no central government."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrimermanPerry1966494_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrimermanPerry1966494-183">[180]</a></sup> Another common criticism of anarchism is that it fits a world of isolation in which only the small enough entities can be self-governing; a response would be that major anarchist thinkers advocated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist_federalism" title="Anarchist federalism">anarchist federalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWard200478_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWard200478-184">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>Philosophy lecturer Andrew G. Fiala composed a list of common arguments against anarchism which includes critiques such as that anarchism is innately related to violence and destruction, not only in the pragmatic world, such as at protests, but in the world of ethics as well. Secondly, anarchism is evaluated as unfeasible or utopian since the state cannot be defeated practically. This line of arguments most often calls for political action within the system to reform it. The third argument is that anarchism is self-contradictory as a ruling theory that has no ruling theory. Anarchism also calls for collective action whilst endorsing the autonomy of the individual, hence no collective action can be taken. Lastly, Fiala mentions a critique towards philosophical anarchism of being ineffective (all talk and thoughts) and in the meantime capitalism and bourgeois class remains strong.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFiala2017"4._Objections_and_Replies"_185-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFiala2017"4._Objections_and_Replies"-185'>[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>Philosophical anarchism has met the criticism of members of academia following the release of pro-anarchist books such as <a href="/wiki/A._John_Simmons" title="A. John Simmons">A. John Simmons</a>' <i>Moral Principles and Political Obligations</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlosko1999536_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlosko1999536-186">[183]</a></sup> Law professor William A. Edmundson authored an essay to argue against three major philosophical anarchist principles which he finds fallacious. Edmundson says that while the individual does not owe the state a duty of obedience, this does not imply that anarchism is the inevitable conclusion and the state is still morally legitimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlosko1999536Kristjánsson2000896_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlosko1999536Kristjánsson2000896-187">[184]</a></sup> In <i>The Problem of Political Authority</i>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Huemer" title="Michael Huemer">Michael Huemer</a> defends philosophical anarchism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDagger201835_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDagger201835-188">[185]</a></sup> claiming that "political authority is a moral illusion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERogers2020_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers2020-189">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the earliest criticisms is that anarchism defies and fails to understand the biological inclination to authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerguson1886_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerguson1886-190">[187]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Raz" title="Joseph Raz">Joseph Raz</a> states that the acceptance of authority implies the belief that following their instructions will afford more success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGans199237_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGans199237-191">[188]</a></sup> Raz believes that this argument is true in following both authorities' successful and mistaken instruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGans199238_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGans199238-192">[189]</a></sup> Anarchists reject this criticism because challenging or disobeying authority does not entail the disappearance of its advantages by acknowledging authority such as doctors or lawyers as reliable, nor does it involve a complete surrender of independent judgment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGans199234,_38_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGans199234,_38-193">[190]</a></sup> Anarchist perception of human nature, rejection of the state, and commitment to social revolution has been criticised by academics as naive, overly simplistic, and unrealistic, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrinn2020206_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrinn2020206-194">[191]</a></sup> Classical anarchism has been criticised for relying too heavily on the belief that the abolition of the state will lead to human cooperation prospering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucy2020162_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucy2020162-148">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>, considered to be one of the principal founders of <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, criticised anarchism's anti-authoritarianism as inherently counter-revolutionary because in his view a revolution is by itself authoritarian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETucker1978_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETucker1978-195">[192]</a></sup> Academic <a href="/wiki/John_Molyneux_(academic)" title="John Molyneux (academic)">John Molyneux</a> writes in his book <i>Anarchism: A Marxist Criticism</i> that "anarchism cannot win", believing that it lacks the ability to properly implement its ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDodds2011_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDodds2011-196">[193]</a></sup> The Marxist criticism of anarchism is that it has a utopian character because all individuals should have anarchist views and values. According to the Marxist view, that a social idea would follow directly from this human ideal and out of the free will of every individual formed its essence. Marxists state that this contradiction was responsible for their inability to act. In the anarchist vision, the conflict between liberty and equality was resolved through coexistence and intertwining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaárFalinaJanowskiKopeček2016488_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaárFalinaJanowskiKopeček2016488-197">[194]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <i>Anarchism: From Theory to Practice</i> (1970),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuérin197012_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuérin197012-14">[14]</a></sup> anarchist historian <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Gu%C3%A9rin" title="Daniel Guérin">Daniel Guérin</a> described it as a synonym for <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_socialism" title="Libertarian socialism">libertarian socialism</a>, and wrote that anarchism "is really a synonym for socialism. The anarchist is primarily a socialist whose aim is to abolish the exploitation of man by man. Anarchism is only one of the streams of socialist thought, that stream whose main components are concern for liberty and haste to abolish the State."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArvidsson2017_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArvidsson2017-15">[15]</a></sup> In his many works on anarchism, such as <i>Chomsky on Anarchism</i> (2005),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChomsky2005123_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChomsky2005123-16">[16]</a></sup> historian <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a> describes anarchism, alongside <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Marxism" title="Libertarian Marxism">libertarian Marxism</a>, as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libertarian" title="Libertarian">libertarian</a> wing of <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOtero1994617_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOtero1994617-17">[17]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Herbert_L._Osgood" title="Herbert L. Osgood">Herbert L. Osgood</a> claimed that anarchism is "the extreme antithesis" of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authoritarian_communism" title="Authoritarian communism">authoritarian communism</a> and <a href="/wiki/State_socialism" title="State socialism">state socialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsgood18891_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsgood18891-19">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Marshall_(author)" title="Peter Marshall (author)">Peter Marshall</a> states that "[i]n general anarchism is closer to socialism than liberalism. ... Anarchism finds itself largely in the socialist camp, but it also has outriders in liberalism. It cannot be reduced to socialism, and is best seen as a separate and distinctive doctrine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarshall1992641_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarshall1992641-11">[11]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Jennings" title="Jeremy Jennings">Jeremy Jennings</a>, "[i]t is hard not to conclude that these ideas", referring to <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-capitalism" title="Anarcho-capitalism">anarcho-capitalism</a>, "are described as anarchist only on the basis of a misunderstanding of what anarchism is." Jennings adds that "anarchism does not stand for the untrammelled freedom of the individual (as the 'anarcho-capitalists' appear to believe) but, as we have already seen, for the extension of individuality and community."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJennings1999147_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJennings1999147-24">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Walter" title="Nicolas Walter">Nicolas Walter</a> wrote that "anarchism does derive from liberalism and socialism both historically and ideologically. ... In a sense, anarchists always remain liberals and socialists, and whenever they reject what is good in either they betray anarchism itself. ... We are liberals but more so, and socialists but more so."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalter200244_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalter200244-22">[21]</a></sup> Michael Newman includes anarchism as one of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist_traditions" title="Socialist traditions">socialist traditions</a>, especially the more socialist-aligned tradition following Proudhon and <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewman200515_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewman200515-20">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brian_Morris_(anthropologist)" title="Brian Morris (anthropologist)">Brian Morris</a> argues that it is "conceptually and historically misleading" to "create a dichotomy between socialism and anarchism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris201564_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris201564-21">[20]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">One common definition adopted by anarchists is that anarchism is a cluster of political philosophies opposing <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hierarchical_organisation" title="Hierarchical organisation">hierarchical organisation</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_capitalism" title="Anarchism and capitalism">capitalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_and_nationalism" title="Anarchism and nationalism">nationalism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a>, and all associated <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutions</a>, in the conduct of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_relations" title="Human relations">human relations</a> in favour of a society based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decentralisation" title="Decentralisation">decentralisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freedom" title="Freedom">freedom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Voluntary_association" title="Voluntary association">voluntary association</a>. Scholars highlight that this definition has the same shortcomings as the definition based on anti-authoritarianism (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_posteriori" title="A posteriori">a posteriori</a></i> conclusion), anti-statism (anarchism is much more than that),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcLaughlin2007166Jun2009507Franks2013386–388_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLaughlin2007166Jun2009507Franks2013386–388-27">[25]</a></sup> and etymology (negation of rulers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcLaughlin200725–29Long2013217_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcLaughlin200725–29Long2013217-28">[26]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
